در تلویح
الفقه هو الأدله الأربعة ولا يبحث فيه عن أحوال الأحكام ، بل إنما يحتاج إلى تصورها ليتمكن من إثباتها ونفيها لكن الصحيح أن موضوعه الأدلة والأحكام ؛ لأنا رجعنا الأدلة بالتعميم إلى الأربعة، والأحكام إلى الخمسة ، ونظرنا في المباحث المتعلقة بكيفية إثبات الأدلة للأحكام إجمالا فوجدنا بعضها راجعة إلى أحوال الأدلة ، وبعضها راجعة إلى أحوال الأحكام ، كما ذكره المصنف في تحصيل القضية الكلية التي يتوصل بها إلى الفقه فجعل أحدهما من المقاصد وآخر من اللواحق . تحكم غاية ما في الباب أن مباحث الأدلة أكثر وأهم لكنه لا يقتضى الأصالة والاستقلال
تشریح
علامه آمدی کی تردید کرنا چاہتے ہیں الا حکام کتاب کے مصنف علامہ آمدی کی رائے یہ ہے کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع ادلہ ہیں ، احکام نہیں۔ احکام کو صرف ذہن نشین کرنا ہوتا ہے تا کہ ان کی ادلہ سے نفی یا اثبات کر سکیں ۔ شارح علامہ آمدی کا رد کرتے ہوئے فرماتے ہیں کہ صحیح بات یہ ہے کہ موضوع ادلہ اور احکام دونوں ہیں، کیونکہ ہم نے جب تمیم کی تو ادلہ کو چار اور احکام کو پانچ وجوب، حرمت، کراهته ، ندب اور اباحت پایا، اس کے بعد اجمالی طور پر ادلہ کے احکام کو ثابت کرنے کی کیفیت پر نظر کی تو بعض احوال کا تعلق ادلہ سے پایا اور بعض کا تعلق احکام سے جیسا کہ مصنف ماتن نے قضیہ کلیہ کے حصول میں ذکر کیا ہے لہذا جب احوال کا تعلق دونوں سے ہوا تو ایک کو مقاصد میں سے بنانا اور دوسرے کو لواحق میں سے سمجھنا تریج بلا مریج اور سینہ زوری ہے۔ زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ کہہ سکتے ہیں کہ ادلہ کے مباحث اکثر اور اہم ہیں اور احکام کے مباحث قلیل ہیں لیکن یہ تو درست نہیں اولہ کو تو اصل اور موضوع ٹھراؤ اور احکام کو درمیان سے نکال کر باہر کرو۔
Usul al-fiqh (discusses) the four proofs and does not investigate the conditions of rulings; rather it needs to conceive them to be able to prove or negate them. But the correct (view) is that its subject is proofs and rulings; because we have limited the proofs by generalization to four, and the rulings to five, and we have examined the discussions related to the manner of proving rulings by proofs in general, and found some of them relating to the states of proofs, and some relating to the states of rulings, as the author mentioned in obtaining the universal proposition by which one reaches fiqh, so he made one of them from the objectives and the other from the appendices. The most that can be said is that discussions of proofs are more numerous and important, but that does not necessitate originality and independence. Commentary: He wants to refute Allamah Amidi, the author of al-Ihkam, whose opinion is that the subject of usul al-fiqh is proofs only, not rulings. Rulings only need to be mentally conceived so that they can be negated or affirmed by proofs. The commentator refutes Amidi, saying that the correct view is that the subject is both proofs and rulings, because when we made an induction, we found the proofs to be four and the rulings to be five: obligation, prohibition, disapproval, recommendation, and permissibility. Then we looked at the manner of proving rulings by proofs in general, and found some conditions relate to proofs and some to rulings, as the author (matn) mentioned in obtaining the universal proposition. Therefore, when the conditions relate to both, making one of them a main objective and the other an appendage is unjustified preference and coercion. At most one can say that discussions of proofs are more numerous and important, but it is not correct to make the first as the origin and subject and expel the rulings.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a dispute regarding the subject matter (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh. The commentator is refuting the view of al-Amidi, who held that the subject is only the proofs (adillah), and that rulings (ahkam) are merely conceived in the mind to be proven or negated. The correct view, as argued here, is that both adillah and ahkam constitute the subject. The reasoning is inductive: by surveying the field, we see that the proofs are four (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) and the rulings are five (wajib, haram, makruh, mandub, mubah). Furthermore, when examining how proofs establish rulings, we find some considerations relate to the proofs themselves (e.g., generality, frequency) and others to the rulings (e.g., being an act of worship or a transaction). Therefore, both are integral. The author (matn) had distinguished between 'objectives' (maqasid) and 'appendages' (lawaḥiq), but the commentator emphasizes that this does not mean one is the subject and the other is not. At best, proof-discussions are more numerous and important, but that does not justify excluding rulings from the subject. This is a classic Hanafi usul position that includes both dimensions.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu‘): The subject matter of a science; what it investigates
أدلة (adillah): The four proofs: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas
أحكام (ahkam): The five legal values: wajib, haram, makruh, mandub, mubah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct subject matter of usul al-fiqh?
A) Only the four proofs (adillah)
B) Only the five rulings (ahkam)
C) Both proofs and rulings
D) Neither proofs nor rulings

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of usul al-fiqh includes both the proofs (adillah) and the rulings (ahkam), as evidenced by the inductive survey of the field.

The Function of Dalil: Indicator, Not Cause
Definitional clarification of 'dalil' as an indicator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'dalil' in Hanafi usul, explain its function as an indicator of the hukm, and distinguish it from a cause that creates the ruling.

Core Idea
The proof gives knowledge of the ruling; it does not bring the ruling into existence.

This lesson examines a key clarification in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: what exactly does a legal proof (dalil) do? The commentator refutes an overly complex distinction by affirming that all proofs are indicators (imarat) that yield knowledge, not causes that effect the ruling. This understanding simplifies many discussions and anchors the epistemological role of proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's distinction
The matn argued that if hukm is eternal, proof gives knowledge of it; if temporal, proof establishes it. The commentator finds this unnecessary.
2
Understand the commentator's core objection
Proofs are identifiers, not creators; their function is to give knowledge regardless of the nature of the ruling.
3
Define 'dalil' as per the commentator
'Dalil' is what gives 'ilm (certain or probable) of the existence or non-existence of something. It does not cause existence.
4
Apply the broad meaning of 'ilm
'Ilm includes both certainty (qat'i) and predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann), so the definition covers all types of proofs.

قال الشارح : " قوله : " إن أريد بـالـحـكـم " هذا كلام لا حاصل له ، لأن الأدلة الشرعية معرفات وإمارات ، ولو سلم أنها أدلة حقيقة ، فلا معنى للدليل إلا ما يفيد العلم بثبوت الشئ أو انتفائه . غاية ما في الباب أن العلم يؤخذ بمعنى الإدراك الحازم او الراجح ليعم القطعى والظني ، فيصح في جميع الأدلة ، وهذا لا يتفاوت بقدم الحكم وحدوثه ، وقد أضطر إلى ذلك إلى آخر الأمر . وليس معنى الدليل ما يفيد نفس الثبوت كما هو شان العلل الخارجية وإن جعلنا الحكم حادثا على ما يشعر به كلامه.
تشریح
ماتن نے کہا تھا کہ اگر حکم سے خطاب اللہ مراد ہو جو قدیم ہے تو پھر ادلہ سے ثابت ہونے کا مطلب ہوگا علم ثبوت الحکم کیونکہ خود حکم دلیل سے ثابت نہیں ہو سکتا اس لیے کہ قدیم ہے اور اگر حکم سے مراد اصطلاح فقہاء ہو وجوب اور حرمت تو پھر خود حکم کا ثبوت مراد ہو گا۔ اب یہاں سے شارح ماتن کی تردید کرنا چاہتے ہیں کہ اس قدر تطویل کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ، اس لیے کہ ادلہ شرعیہ معرفات اور امارات ہیں جن سے حکم کا علم حاصل ہوتا ہے، خود حکم ثابت نہیں ہوتا اور اگر ان کو ادلہ حقیقہ تسلیم بھی کر لیا جائے تو ہم کہتے ہیں کہ دلیل وہ ہوتی ہے جس سے علم بثبوت الشئ یا انتفاء الشئ حاصل ہو تو اس سے حکم کے قدیم یا حادث ہونے سے فرق نہیں پڑتا ۔ اور اگر حکم کو حادث مانیں تو پھر بھی دلیل سے اس کا علم حاصل ہوگا نہ کہ دلیل حکم کو ثابت کرے گی، زیادہ سے زیادہ علم کو عام معنی میں لینا پڑے گا کہ علم بثبوت الحکم سے مراد عام ہے الادراك الحازم یعنی یقین یا الراجح یعنی نلمن غالب اور ماتن بھی بالآ خر اسی بات کی طرف مجبور ہوا ہے اور کہا ہے کہ فنقول : نريد في جميع ذلك إثبات العلم لنا أو غلبة الظن تو خواہ مخواہ اتنی طوالت کرنے کی ضرورت کیا ہے۔
بالآخر
The commentator said: 'His statement: “If what is meant by hukm is…” this is a pointless statement, because the legal proofs are identifiers and indicators. Even if it is conceded that they are proofs in reality, the meaning of proof is nothing other than what gives knowledge of the existence or non-existence of a thing. The most that can be said is that knowledge is taken in the sense of firm perception or preponderant, to include certain and probable, so it applies to all proofs, and this does not differ by the eternality or temporality of the ruling. And he was compelled to that [acknowledgement] eventually. The meaning of proof is not that it itself gives existence, as is the nature of external causes, even if we consider the ruling to be temporal as his statement suggests.' Commentary: The author (matn) had said that if by hukm is meant the address of Allah which is eternal, then proving by proofs would mean knowledge of the existence of the hukm, because the hukm itself cannot be proven by a proof since it is eternal. And if by hukm is meant the jurists' terminology of obligation and prohibition, then the proof establishes the hukm itself. Now the commentator refutes the author, saying there is no need for such length, because the legal proofs are identifiers and indicators by which knowledge of the hukm is obtained, not that the hukm itself is proven. Even if they are accepted as real proofs, we say that a proof is that which gives knowledge of the existence or non-existence of a thing, and this does not differ whether the hukm is eternal or temporal. And if we consider the hukm to be temporal, then still the proof gives knowledge of it, not that the proof establishes it. At most, knowledge must be taken in a general sense that 'knowledge of the existence of the hukm' means firm perception or preponderant belief. And the author was also eventually compelled to this, as he said: 'We mean in all that the establishment of knowledge for us or preponderant belief.' So what need was there for all this length? Finally.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the term 'dalil' (proof) in usul al-fiqh. The commentator argues against an overly elaborate distinction made by the author (matn) regarding whether the hukm is eternal (qadim) or temporal (hadith). The key point is that the legal proofs (adillah shar'iyyah) are indicators (imarat) that give knowledge (ilm) of the ruling, not that they cause the ruling's existence. The ruling itself—whether it is Allah's eternal speech or a temporal legal value—is not brought into being by the proof. The proof merely informs us of its existence or non-existence. 'Knowledge' here is understood broadly to include both certainty (qat'i) and predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann). Therefore, the nature of the ruling (eternal vs. created) does not affect the function of the proof. The commentator concludes that the author eventually conceded this point when he said the proof establishes knowledge or strong belief. Thus, the definition of dalil is: that which yields knowledge (or strong probability) of the existence or non-existence of something.

Key Terms
دليل (dalil): That which gives knowledge (certain or probable) of the existence or non-existence of something
حكم (hukm): The legal ruling (e.g., obligation, prohibition) or the divine address
علم (‘ilm): Firm perception or preponderant belief, including both certainty and strong probability

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the primary function of a legal proof (dalil)?
A) To bring the ruling into existence
B) To give knowledge of the existence or non-existence of the ruling
C) To change the ruling from eternal to temporal
D) To create certainty only, not probability

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a legal proof (dalil) is an indicator that gives knowledge of the hukm, not a cause that brings it into being.

Can a Science Have Multiple Subjects? – The Dispute in Usul al-Fiqh
Disputing the matn's claim about the singularity of a science's subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a science can have multiple subjects according to Hanafi usul, and evaluate the critique of the opposing view.

Core Idea
A science may have multiple subjects if its investigation involves an idafa and relevant attributes arise from both sides.

In this lesson, we examine a key methodological debate in Hanafi usul: can a single science ('ilm) have more than one subject (mawdu')? The commentator (sharh) challenges the matn's insistence on a single subject, presenting a nuanced position based on the nature of the relation (idafa) underlying the science's investigation. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to define the scope of any discipline and recognize legitimate ikhtilaf within the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the matn's position
Read the matn's argument: a science must have one subject. If the mabhuth 'anhu is an idafa, the subject may be one or both depending on attributes; if not idafa, the subject is one. Note the claim that multiple subjects lead to multiple sciences.
The matn is Sadr al-Shariah, a Hanafi authority.
2
Identify the commentator's critique
Locate where the sharh says 'fi nazar' (there is consideration). He rejects the matn's inference that multiplicity of issues (masa'il) necessitates multiple sciences, using the example of usul al-fiqh where attributes arise from both evidences and rulings.
The sharh is likely from the Talweeh tradition.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: does usul al-fiqh have one subject (as matn) or two (evidences and rulings)? The sharh's criterion allows two subjects, which affects how the discipline is structured.
This ikhtilaf is about defining the boundaries of a science.

The commentator said: "His statement: 'Know that these are three investigations concerning the subject (mawdu). He cited them in disagreement with the majority of verifiers. The observer who is aware of the statements of the scholars in this context would be astonished. First: The absolute statement permitting the multiplicity of the subject, even if more than two, is not correct. Rather, the investigation is that the sought-after (mabhuth 'anhu) in a science is either an idafa (relation) between two things or not. In the first case, either the attributes that have a role in the sought-after are some arising from one of the two parts of the relation (al-mudafayn) and some from the other, or not. If that is the case, then the subject of the science is both parts of the relation, as in usul where the investigation is about establishing evidences for rulings, and the states that have a role in that are some arising from the evidence, like generality (umum), commonality (ishtirak), and tawatur, and some from the ruling, like its being an act of worship or a penalty; so its subject is both evidences and rulings. But if the sought-after is not an idafa, as in fiqh which investigates the obligation of an action of the mukallaf and its prohibition etc., or it is an idafa but the states arising from one of the two parts have no role in that, as the author assumed previously, then the subject is not one, because difference of subject necessitates difference of issues, which necessitates difference of the science, necessarily, because a science only differs by differing in its known objects (ma'lumat), which are the issues." Commentary: From here, the author wants to transmit the matn's investigation about the subject and then refute it. He says: The author (matn) said something concerning the subject that is completely contrary to the majority, and whoever is familiar with the statements of the majority would be astonished. The summary is that the matn wrote that a science having two or more subjects is incorrect. Rather, a science can only have one subject. Because the ma'dan (sought-after) in a science is either an idafa or not. If it is idafa, like usul al-fiqh, then we look at the states that relate to the ma'dan: if some relate to the mudaf and some to the mudaf ilayh, then both are subjects, as we see here that some states relate to evidences and some to rulings, so both are subjects to avoid arbitrary preference. If the ma'dan is an idafa but the states relate only to one of the two parts, then the subject is one, like in logic where the subject is the conveying of tasawwur and tasdiq to an unknown tasawwur and tasdiq, but the states relate only to the mudaf (the conveyer) so the subject is the conveying tasawwur and tasdiq, not the destination. And if there is no idafa, then the subject is one, as in fiqh where the subject is wujub and hurma. Because a science being one or multiple depends on the subject being one or multiple. If a science is one but has multiple subjects, then its issues would be multiple, making it not one but multiple sciences. The commentator said: "And there is consideration (nazar) in this: if by difference of issues is meant mere multiplicity, we do not accept that it necessitates difference of science; it is obvious that the issues of a single science are many indeed. And if by difference is meant lack of proportionality..."
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (munazara) about the subject (mawdu) of a science. The sharh quotes the matn's argument that a science cannot have multiple subjects. The matn had divided the mabhuth 'anhu (what is investigated) into two categories: idafa (relation) or not. If idafa, the subject may be one or both of the related terms depending on whether the relevant attributes arise from both sides or only one. If not idafa, the subject is one. The sharh summarizes the matn's view that multiple subjects would cause multiplicity of issues, leading to multiple sciences. Then the sharh offers a critical note ('fihi nazar'): mere multiplicity of issues does not necessitate multiple sciences, since even a single science has many issues. The sharh implies that the matn's reasoning is flawed. The passage exemplifies the precision of Hanafi usul scholars in defining the scope and subject of each discipline.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu): The subject matter of a science; what is primarily investigated.
اضافة (idafa): A relation between two things (mudaf and mudaf ilayh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the commentator argue that a science can have multiple subjects?
A) Always, regardless of the mabhuth 'anhu.
B) Never; a science must have exactly one subject.
C) When it involves an idafa and attributes arise from both sides of the relation.
D) Only in theoretical sciences like logic.

Key Takeaway
A science's subject may be multiple if its investigation concerns a relation (idafa) and relevant properties originate from both related entities.

The Two Types of 'Haythiyya' in Defining the Subject of a Science
Explaining the two meanings of 'haythiyya' in subject definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of 'haythiyya' in subject definitions and apply the correct interpretation to determine whether an attribute is part of the subject or an exposition of essential accidents.

Core Idea
In defining a science, the 'haythiyya' can be part of the subject or an exposition of attributes to be studied.

This lesson explores a key epistemological concept in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: how the 'aspect' (haythiyya) functions in defining the subject matter of a science. The commentators clarify that sometimes the aspect is an integral part of the subject itself, while other times it merely points to the attributes under investigation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly delineating the scope of any science, including usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the linguistic background of 'haythiyya'
The word 'hayth' originally means 'place' and is used metaphorically for 'aspect' or 'consideration'. Recognize that when a science is defined by 'min haythu kaza', it refers to the perspective from which the subject is studied.
This metaphorical usage is common in Islamic sciences.
2
Identify the two types of 'haythiyya'
According to the author (Sadr al-Shariah), there are two types: (1) 'haythiyya' as part of the subject (e.g., metaphysics: existence from the perspective of existence), and (2) 'haythiyya' as exposition of the attributes studied (e.g., medicine: the body from the perspective of health and disease).
In type 1, the aspect itself is not investigated; in type 2, the aspect indicates what is investigated.
3
Apply the classification to the examples
Work through the given examples: in metaphysics, existence is the subject, and the 'haythiyya' is part of it, so we study essential accidents (causality, etc.) not existence itself. In medicine, the body is the subject, and the 'haythiyya' of health/disease is the very attributes studied.
This affects what counts as a valid subject definition.
4
Analyze the objection
An objector argues that in both cases, 'haythiyya' could be a restriction (qayd) on the subject rather than a part. For metaphysics, existence restricted by 'as existence'; for medicine, body restricted by 'as health/disease'. This avoids the conclusion that two sciences share the same subject in essence.
The objection is methodological: does the science investigate its own subject or its qualifications?
5
Evaluate the implications
Reflect on why this matters for usul al-fiqh: defining the subject of usul al-fiqh (the four evidences) and whether it is one science or many. The correct understanding of 'haythiyya' helps structure scientific inquiry.
The passage opens with an example from usul al-fiqh and logic.

Source Text

ہیں، مثلا : اصول فقہ کا موضوع دلیل کا مفہوم کلی نہیں بلکہ دلیل کے افراد ہیں یعنی کتاب سنت، اجماع اور قیاس پھر اصول فقہ کو ایک علم نہیں کہنا چاہیے کیونکہ اس کا موضوع چار چیزیں ہیں ۔ اسی طرح منطق کا موضوع تصور و تصدیق کا مفہوم نہیں بلکہ ان کے افراد جنس، نوع، فصل، قیاس، استقراء اور تمثیل وغیرہ پھر ہیں تو منطق کو ایک علم شمار کرنے غلط ہو گا۔

قال الشارح: قوله : ومنها أنه قد تذكر الحيثية المذكورة في الموضوع حيث يقال : موضوع هذ العلم هو ذلك الشئ من حيث كذا ولفظ "حيث "موضوع للمكان أستعير لجهة الشئ واعتباره يقال : موضوع هذا الموجود من حيث هو موجود أى : من هذه الجهة وبهذه الاعتبار . والحيثية المذكورة في الموضوع قد لا تكون من الأعراض المبحوث عنها في العلم . كقولهم: موضوع العلم الآلهي الباحث عن أحوال الموجودات المحردة هو الموجود من حيث أنه موجود بمعنى أنه يبحث عن العوارض التي تلحق الموجود من حيث أنه موجود لا من حيث أنه جوهر أو عرض أو جسم أو مجرد وذلك كالعلية ، والمعلولية ، والوجوب ، والإمكان ، والقدم ، والحدوث ونحو ذلك، ولا يبحث فيه عن حيثية الوجود ، إذ لا معنى لإثباتها للموجود ؛ وقد تكون من الأعراض المبحوث عنها في العلم ، كقولهم : موضوع الطب بدن الإنسان من حيث أنه يصح ويمرض ، وموضوع الطبيعي : الجسم من حيث أنه يتحرك ، ويسكن ، والصحة ، والمرض ، من الأعراض الذاتية المبحوث عنها في الطب ، وكذا الحركة والسكون في الطبيعي . فذهب المصنف إلى أن الحيثية في القسم الأول من الموضوع ، وفي الثاني بيان للأعراض الذاتية المبحوث عنها في العلم إذ لو كانت جزءا من الموضوع كما في القسم الأول لما صح أن يبحث عنها في العلم ويجعل من محمولات مسائله إذ لا يبحث في العلم عن أجزاء الموضوع بل عن أعراضه الذاتية .

تشریح
یہاں سے ماتن کی دوسری تحقیق (جو موضوع کے بارے میں بیان کی گئی ) کا مطلب واضح کر رہے ہیں۔ فرماتے ہیں کہ عام طور پر موضوع کے ساتھ "حیث" کا لفظ ذکر کیا جاتا ہے حیث کا لغت میں معنی "مکان" کے آتا ہے جیسا کہ حيث تقعد أقعد پھر اس کو مجاز ایہہ اور اعتبار کے معنی میں استعمال کیا جانے لگا جیسے کہا جاتا ہے الموجود من حيث الموجود والإنسان من حيث الإنسان یعنی بہتہ اور باعتبار کے معنی میں آتا ہے پھر حیثیت کو جب موضوع کے ساتھ ذکر کیا جائے کبھی تو وہ موضوع کے لیے کالجزء ہوتی ہے لہذا اس وقت اس سے بحث نہ کی جائے گی بلکہ اس حیثیت سے لگنے والے اعراض سے بحث کی جائے گی، کیونکہ قانون ہے کہ خود موضوع اور اس کے اجزاء مفروغ عنہ ہوتے ہیں، مثلا: کہا جاتا ہے کہ علم الہی کا موضوع موجود من حيث هو موجود اب یہاں پر خود وجود سے بحث نہ ہوگی بلکہ اس حیثیت سے جو اس کو عوارضات لگیں گے مثلا علیت ، معلولیت ، وجوب امکان اور قدم وغیرہ سے بحث ہوگی اور کبھی حیثیت بیان اعراض کے لیے ہوتی ہے جیسے کہ علم الطب کا موضوع بدن من حيث الصحة والمرض اب یہاں پر یہ مطلب نہیں کہ اس حیثیت سے جو اور اعراض لگیں ان سے بحث ہو خود صحت و مرض سے نہ ہو، بلکہ مطلب یہ ہے کہ خود صحت اور مرض سے بحث ہو گی کیونکہ یہاں پر حیثیت بیان کے لیے ہے تو اگر حیثیت بیانیہ نہ ہوتی تو ہم صحت اور مرض سے بحث نہ کر سکتے۔

قال الشارح : " ولقائل أن يقول : لا يسلم أنها في الأول جزء من الموضوع ، بل قيد لموضوعيته بمعنى أن البحث عن الأعراض الذاتية التي تلحق من تلك الحيثية وبذلك الاعتبار . وعلى هذا لو جعلنا الحيثية في القسم الثاني أيضا قيدا للموضوع على ما هو ظاهر كلام القوم لا بيانا للأعراض الذاتية على ما ذهب إليه المصنف لم يكن البحث عنها في العلم بحثا عن أجزاء الموضوع ، ولم يلزم منها ما لزم المصنف من تشارك العلمين في موضوع واحد بالذات والاعتبار

تشریح
ماتن کی تحقیق پر اعتراض کرنا مقصود ہے کہ ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ علم الہی کے موضوع میں حیثیت جزء ہے، بلکہ قید ہے، کیونکہ اگر جزء بنا ئیں تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہو گا کہ علم الہی کا موضوع مجموعہ موجود
For example: The subject of Usul al-Fiqh is not the universal concept of evidence but rather the individual evidences—namely the Book, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas. Therefore, Usul al-Fiqh should not be called one science because its subject is four things. Similarly, the subject of Logic is not the universal concept of concept and assent but rather their individuals—genus, species, differentia, syllogism, induction, and example. So if they are many, then counting Logic as one science would be wrong.

The commentator said: His statement: Among them is that the aforementioned 'haythiyya' is mentioned in the subject, where it is said: The subject of this science is that thing from the perspective of such-and-such. The word 'hayth' is originally used for place, then borrowed for aspect and consideration. For example, it is said: The subject of this existent from the perspective that it exists, meaning from this aspect and this consideration. The 'haythiyya' mentioned in the subject may not be among the attributes investigated in the science. As in their saying: The subject of metaphysics (al-ilahiyyat), which investigates the states of immaterial existents, is existence from the perspective that it exists, meaning it investigates the accidents that attach to existence as existence, not as substance, accident, body, or immaterial—such as causality, effect, necessity, possibility, eternity, origination, etc. It does not investigate the 'haythiyya' of existence itself, as there is no meaning to affirming it for existence. And it may be among the attributes investigated in the science, as in their saying: The subject of medicine is the human body from the perspective that it is healthy and sick; and the subject of physics is the body from the perspective that it moves and rests. Health and illness are essential attributes investigated in medicine, and likewise motion and rest in physics. The author holds that the 'haythiyya' in the first section is part of the subject, while in the second it is an exposition of the essential attributes investigated in the science. For if it were part of the subject, as in the first section, it would not be valid to investigate it in the science and make it a predicate of its propositions, since the science does not investigate parts of the subject but rather its essential attributes.

Explanation: Here the meaning of the author's second investigation (concerning the subject) is being clarified. He says: Usually the word 'hayth' is mentioned with the subject. Lexically, 'hayth' means 'place', as in 'where you sit, I sit'. Then it came to be used metaphorically in the sense of 'aspect' and 'consideration', as in 'al-mawjud min haythu huwa mawjud' and 'al-insan min haythu huwa insan'—meaning 'from the aspect and by consideration'. When 'haythiyya' is mentioned with the subject, sometimes it is like a part of the subject, so it is not investigated; rather, the accidents that attach from that aspect are investigated, because the rule is that the subject itself and its parts are given (musallam). For example, it is said: The subject of metaphysics is existence from the perspective that it exists. Now here existence itself is not investigated, but rather the accidents that attach to it from that aspect—like causality, effect, necessity, possibility, eternity, etc. And sometimes the 'haythiyya' is an exposition of the attributes, as in the subject of medicine: the body from the perspective of health and illness. Here it does not mean that from that aspect other attributes attach and we investigate those, but not health and illness themselves. Rather, it means that we investigate health and illness themselves, because here the 'haythiyya' is for exposition. If it were not expositive, we could not investigate health and illness.

The commentator said: And it may be objected: It is not granted that in the first section it is a part of the subject; rather, it is a restriction (qayd) for its being a subject, meaning that the investigation is about the essential attributes that attach from that aspect and by that consideration. And if we make the 'haythiyya' in the second section also a restriction for the subject, as is apparent from the statements of the scholars, not an exposition of the essential attributes as the author held, then investigating them in the science would not be an investigation into parts of the subject, nor would there follow what the author concluded about two sciences sharing one subject in essence and consideration.

Explanation: The intention is to object to the author's investigation: We do not concede that in the subject of metaphysics the 'haythiyya' is a part; rather, it is a restriction. Because if we make it a part, then it would mean that the subject of metaphysics is the composite of existence
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the concept of 'haythiyya' (the aspect or perspective) in defining the subject matter of a science, a key topic in usul al-fiqh and Islamic epistemology. The text opens with an example from logic and usul al-fiqh to illustrate that the subject of a science is not a universal concept but the individuals that fall under it. Then the commentator (likely Taftazani in Al-Talwih) explains the statement of the matn (Al-Tawdih by Sadr al-Shariah) about 'haythiyya'.

First, he explains the linguistic origin: 'hayth' means 'place' and is used metaphorically for 'aspect' or 'consideration'. When we say 'the subject of metaphysics is existence from the perspective that it exists', the phrase 'from the perspective that it exists' (min haythu huwa mawjud) can have two interpretations. The author (Sadr al-Shariah) holds that in this case, the 'haythiyya' is part of the subject itself. Therefore, we do not investigate existence qua existence; rather, we investigate the essential attributes that arise from existence, such as causality, necessity, etc. This is because the subject and its parts are taken as given (musallam) and not subject to investigation.

Second, in the example of medicine, 'the human body from the perspective that it is healthy or sick', the 'haythiyya' is not a part of the subject but an exposition (bayan) of the attributes to be investigated. Here, health and illness themselves are the essential attributes studied in medicine. If the 'haythiyya' were part of the subject, we could not study health and illness themselves.

Then an objection is raised: Why consider the 'haythiyya' in the first case a part? Instead, it could be a restriction (qayd) for the subject, meaning the subject is existence qualified by being considered as such. And in the second case, it could also be a restriction, not an exposition. This would avoid the problem of two sciences sharing the same subject in essence and consideration. The objector proposes that in both cases, 'haythiyya' is a restriction on the subject, not a part. This objection reflects a nuanced debate in Islamic epistemology about how to define the subject matter of sciences.

Key Terms
حيثية (haythiyya): The aspect or perspective from which something is considered; in subject definitions, it can be either a part of the subject (first type) or an exposition of the essential attributes to be studied (second type).
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter of a science; that which the science studies and about which it investigates essential attributes.
عرض ذاتي (arad dhati): Essential accident or attribute that is necessarily attached to the subject and constitutes the main objects of investigation in a science.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the difference between the 'haythiyya' in the definition of metaphysics and medicine?
A) In metaphysics, the 'haythiyya' is studied; in medicine, it is not.
B) In metaphysics, the 'haythiyya' is part of the subject; in medicine, it is an exposition of the attributes studied.
C) Both are the same: the 'haythiyya' is a restriction on the subject.
D) The 'haythiyya' is irrelevant in both definitions.

Key Takeaway
The 'haythiyya' in a subject definition can either be part of the subject (not investigated) or an exposition of the attributes to be studied, and this distinction is crucial for correctly delimiting a science.

Method of Learning
advanced
10 min
The Haythiyya: Part of the Subject or Restriction? A Usul Dispute
Analyzing and critiquing the matn's view on haythiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on haythiyya in defining a science's subject, apply the commentator's critique, and evaluate which position avoids logical overlap.

Core Idea
Haythiyya restricts the inquiry, not the subject—preventing sciences from overlapping.

In defining the subject-matter of a science, the phrase 'min haythu' (inasmuch as) is often added. Is this phrase part of the subject itself, or does it merely restrict the accidents studied? The matn of Sadr al-Shariah distinguishes two cases: in Ilahiyyat it is part of the subject, in medicine it is explanatory. The commentator (Al-Talwih) objects, arguing that in both cases it is a restriction (qayd). This disagreement has consequences for whether two sciences can share the same subject. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the famous objection of circularity.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the two uses of haythiyya in the matn
The matn holds that in Ilahiyyat, haythiyya is like a part of the subject; in medicine, it is explanatory of the accidents studied. Recognize that this leads to different treatment of the subject.
In Ilahiyyat, the subject is 'existent inasmuch as it is existent'—the haythiyya is integral.
2
2. State the commentator's critique
The commentator argues that haythiyya is not a part but a restriction in both cases. If it were a part, the subject would be composite and all accidents would attach to that composite, which is false. If it were explanatory in medicine, the unrestricted subject (body) would be shared by multiple sciences, which is invalid.
The commentator follows the majority (jumhur) in making it a restriction everywhere.
3
3. Understand the circularity objection
If haythiyya is a restriction, then the attributes mentioned (e.g., health and disease) would themselves not be the studied accidents—they would be the perspective for studying other accidents. But health and disease are precisely what is studied in medicine, leading to circularity: that by which something attaches cannot itself attach.
The example: health and disease do not attach to the body 'inasmuch as it is healthy/ill'.
4
4. Examine the resolution
The well-known answer is that 'min hayth' means 'in terms of the possibility of health and disease' (or readiness). The verified position (tahqiq) is that haythiyya indicates the universal perspective under which the science investigates the subject's essential accidents—it does not mean all studied accidents attach via that haythiyya.
Thus, health and disease are studied, but the inquiry is conducted under the aspect of health/disease.
5
5. Compare the thamarah (consequences)
The matn's view leads to the problem of two sciences sharing one subject (if haythiyya is explanatory) or a composite subject (if it is part). The commentator's view avoids both problems by keeping the subject simple and each science distinct.
The tarjih: the commentator's view is preferred and supported by the majority.

جب موضوع مطلق عن القید ہے تو کئی علوم اس میں شریک ہو گئے اور تشارک فی العلوم باطل ہے تو دونوں جگہ حیثیت کو قید للموضوع بنایا جائے۔ قال الشارح : " ثم يرد الإشكال المشهور وهو أنه يجب أن لا تكون الحيثية لبدن الإنسان من حيث يصح ويمرض ، ولا الحركة والسكون مما يعرض للجسم من حيث يتحرك ويسكن .. والمشهور في الجواب أن المراد من حيث إمكان الصحة ، والمرض ، أو الحركة ، و السكون ، والاستعداد لذلك وهذا ليس من جميع المباحث هذا المعنى الكلى لا على معنى أن جميع العوارض المبحوث عنها يكون لحوقها لموضوعها بواسطة هذه الحيثية ألبتة " . تشریح حیثیت کو تقییدیہ بنانے کی صورت میں تشارك علوم في موضوع واحد والا اعتراض تو لازم نہیں آئے گا البتہ ایک اور اشکال مشہور وارد ہو گا۔ اشکال یہ ہے کہ آپ نے جب علم طب میں من حيث الصحة والمرض کو قید بنایا یا علم طبعی میں من حيث الحركة والسكون کو قید بنایا تو اس وقت لازم آتا ہے کہ خود صحت اور مرض اسی طرح حرکت اور سکون سے بحث نہ ہو بلکہ اس حیثیت سے جو عوارضات
They say: Usually the word 'haythiyya' is mentioned with the subject. Haythu lexically means 'place', as in 'where you sit, I sit'. Then it is used metaphorically to mean 'in respect of' and 'consideration', as is said 'al-mawjud min haythu huwa mawjud' and 'al-insan min haythu huwa insan', i.e., 'by virtue of' and 'by consideration'. Then when haythiyya is mentioned with the subject, sometimes it is like a part of the subject, so then discussion will not be about it but about the accidents that attach from that consideration, because the rule is that the subject itself and its parts are assumed. For example: It is said that the subject of Ilahiyyat is 'existent inasmuch as it is existent'. Here discussion is not about existence itself but about the accidents that attach from that consideration, such as causality, effect, necessity, contingency, eternity, etc. And sometimes haythiyya is for expressing the accidents, as in the subject of medicine is 'the body in terms of health and disease'. Here it does not mean that discussion is about other accidents that attach from that consideration and not about health and disease themselves; rather it means that discussion is about health and disease themselves, because here haythiyya is explanatory. If it were not explanatory, we could not discuss health and disease. The commentator said: "An objector may say: It is not conceded that in the first case it is part of the subject; rather it is a restriction for its subjecthood, meaning that the inquiry is about the essential accidents that attach from that consideration and by that respect. And on this, if we make the haythiyya in the second case also a restriction for the subject, as is apparent from the statement of the majority—not an explanation of the essential accidents as the author held—then the inquiry about them in the science would not be an inquiry about parts of the subject, and there would not follow from it what followed for the author of the two sciences sharing one subject in essence and consideration." Commentary: The purpose is to object to the author's investigation. We do not accept that in Ilahiyyat the haythiyya is a part; rather it is a restriction. Because if we make it a part, that would mean the subject of Ilahiyyat is the composite 'existent inasmuch as it is existent', and all accidents attach to this composite, but this is false. So haythiyya is restrictive. Similarly, in the subject of medicine, if haythiyya is made restrictive, as appears from the statement of the majority, then haythiyya will not be a part for the subject. And in the case of making it explanatory, the objection that follows for the author—that two sciences share one subject—would also be removed, because obviously when the subject is absolute and unrestricted, several sciences share it, and sharing among sciences is invalid. So in both places, haythiyya is made a restriction for the subject. The commentator said: "Then the famous problem arises: it must be that the haythiyya is not among the accidents inquired about in the science, because it is not something that attaches to the subject from itself; otherwise circularity would follow, because that by which something attaches to something must precede the attached. For example, health and disease do not attach to the human body inasmuch as it is healthy and ill, nor do motion and rest attach to the body inasmuch as it moves and rests. The well-known answer is that what is meant is 'in terms of the possibility of health and disease, or motion and rest, and the readiness for that', and this is not among the accidents inquired about in the science. The verified position is that since the subject is that which in a science is investigated regarding its essential accidents, it is restricted by the haythiyya meaning that the inquiry into the accidents is only with respect to the haythiyya and by considering it, i.e., in all investigations this universal meaning is observed—not meaning that all the accidents inquired about attach to their subject by means of this haythiyya absolutely." Commentary: By making haythiyya restrictive, the objection of two sciences sharing one subject does not arise. However, another famous problem occurs: when you make 'in terms of health and disease' a restriction in medicine, or 'in terms of motion and rest' a restriction in physics, then it necessarily follows that discussion is not about health and disease themselves, nor about motion and rest themselves, but about the accidents that attach from that consideration.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the function of 'haythiyya' (consideration/aspect) in defining the subject-matter of a science. The matn (likely Sadr al-Shariah's Al-Tawdih) distinguished two uses of haythiyya: (1) as part of the subject (juz') in Ilahiyyat, where the subject is 'existent inasmuch as it is existent'—the haythiyya is integral to the subject, so inquiry is about accidents attaching to the subject from that aspect; (2) as explanatory (bayaniyya) in sciences like medicine, where the haythiyya merely indicates the attributes to be studied (health and disease), and the subject itself is the body. The commentator (Al-Talwih) first objects: in the first case, haythiyya is not a part but a restriction (qayd), meaning the subject is 'existent' and the inquiry is limited to essential accidents from that aspect. If it were a part, the subject would be a composite and all accidents would attach to that composite, which is false. In the second case, the commentator argues that haythiyya should also be treated as a restriction, not merely explanatory, as the majority (jumhur) hold. This avoids the problem of two sciences sharing the same subject: if the subject is unrestricted, different sciences could study the same thing, which is invalid. By making haythiyya a restriction, each science has a unique subject defined by that restriction. Then the commentator raises a famous problem: if haythiyya is a restriction, then the attributes mentioned (e.g., health and disease) would not be the studied accidents; rather, only accidents attaching from that restriction would be studied. This leads to circularity because the restriction itself cannot be the cause of attachment. The well-known answer is that 'from where' means 'in terms of possibility' or 'readiness', not actual attachment. The verified position (tahqiq) is that haythiyya indicates the universal perspective under which the science investigates the subject's essential accidents; it does not necessarily mean that all studied accidents attach via that haythiyya. This resolution maintains that the studied accidents (like health/disease) are directly investigated, but the inquiry is conducted under the aspect of health/disease. The Urdu commentary clarifies these points and shows the chain of objection and response.

Key Terms
الحيثية (al-haythiyya): The aspect or consideration under which a subject is studied in a science; it may function as part of the subject or as a restriction for the attributes studied.
جزء الموضوع (juz' al-mawdu'): A part of the subject itself, so that the subject is a composite including the consideration.
قيد الموضوعية (qayd al-mawdu'iyya): A restriction on the subjecthood, meaning the inquiry is limited to accidents that attach from that specific consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator (Al-Talwih), why must haythiyya be a restriction (qayd) in both Ilahiyyat and medicine?
A) Because the matn explicitly says so.
B) To avoid a composite subject in Ilahiyyat and subject-sharing in medicine.
C) Because the majority consider it a part.
D) Because the haythiyya itself is an accident to be studied.

Key Takeaway
Haythiyya functions as a restriction on the subjecthood of a science, ensuring each science has a unique, simple subject and logically sound inquiry.

Method of Learning
intermediate
10 min
Haythiyya Objection Resolution and Shared Subjects
Commentary on haythiyya objection and permissibility of multiple sciences sharing a subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the function of haythiyya (aspect) in defining the subject of a science and apply the logical reasoning to argue that multiple sciences can share the same subject.

Core Idea
The haythiyya is a universal guiding concept, not a specific attribute; sciences are distinguished by their predicates, not just their subjects.

This passage from Dars-e-Talweeh tackles two foundational issues in the philosophy of science: how a science's subject is restricted by an aspect (haythiyya) without circularity, and whether different sciences can legitimately share the same subject. These discussions are essential for understanding how Hanafi usul defines the scope and identity of a science, such as fiqh or medicine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize the dilemma: if the subject of a science (e.g., human body) is restricted by an aspect (e.g., from the perspective of health and illness), then that aspect becomes the basis for investigation, but the aspect itself cannot be investigated without circularity (tashrīf al-shay' 'alā nafsih).
This is the 'well-known objection' (al-ishkāl al-mashhūr).
2
Analyze the two solutions
First, interpret the aspect as possibility/readiness (imkān/isti‘dād) rather than actual attributes. Second, treat the aspect as a universal meaning (mafhūm kullī) that guides all discussions without being a particular attribute.
The first is the mashhūr (well-known) answer; the second is the taḥqīq (investigative) answer.
3
Understand the concept of tashāruk al-‘ulūm
The passage argues that multiple sciences can share the same subject (e.g., both medicine and natural philosophy study the human body) because they investigate different essential attributes (a‘rāḍ dhātiyya).
The identity of a science depends on its issues (masā'il) and predicates (maḥmūlāt), not solely on the subject.
4
Apply the logical argument (dalīl ‘aqlī)
The argument uses a form of qiyās: if a single subject has multiple types of essential attributes, it is valid to assign each type to a different science. This proves the permissibility of shared subjects.
The argument is classified as Qiyas Jaliy, with zannī thubūt but qaṭ‘ī dalāla.

بعض أنواعها ، وفى علم آخر عن بعض آخر ، فيتمايز العلمان أو العلوم بالأعراض المبحوث عنه ، وإن اتحد الموضوع وذلك ، لأن اتحاد العلم واختلافه إنما هو بحسب المعلومات ، أعنى المسائل وكما يتحد المسائل باتحاد مـوضـوعـاتـهـا بـأن يرجع الجميع إلى موضوع العلم ، ويختلف باختلافها كذلك بتحد باتحاد محمولاتها بأن يرجع الجميع إلى نوع من الأعراض الذاتية للموضوع ويختلف باختلافها ، فكما اعتبر اختلاف العلوم باختلاف الموضوعات ، يجوز أن يعتبر باختلاف المحمولات بأن يؤخذ موضوع واحد بالذات والاعتبار ويجعل البحث عن أعراضه الذاتية علما ، وعن البعض بالآخر علما آخر فيكون علمين متشاركين في الموضوع
From the perspective that it exists and all accidental properties apply to this aggregate, while this is false, so the restrictive aspect is this. Similarly, in the subject of medicine, if the aspect is made a restriction as is known from the speech of the majority, then the aspect will not be a part of the subject, and in the case of making it an explanatory aspect, the objection ‘sharing of sciences in one subject’ that arises against the author will be removed, because it is obvious that when the subject is unrestricted, several sciences would share it, and sharing in sciences is invalid, so in both places the aspect is made a restriction for the subject. The commentator said: Then the well-known objection is presented: that the aspect must not be among the accidental properties investigated in the science, necessarily because it is not among what applies to the subject in itself, otherwise there would be self-precedence, necessarily because what makes a thing apply to a thing must precede the attribute. For example, health and illness are not what applies to the human body from the perspective of being healthy or ill, nor is motion and rest what applies to the body from the perspective of moving or resting. The well-known answer is that what is meant is the possibility of health and illness, or motion and rest, and readiness for that, and this is not among the accidental properties investigated in the science. The true investigation is that when the subject is that about which the science investigates its essential attributes, it is restricted by the aspect meaning that the investigation of attributes is only with consideration of the aspect and by looking at it, i.e., in all discussions this universal meaning is observed, not meaning that all the attributes investigated attach to their subject necessarily through this aspect. Explanation: In the case of making the aspect a restriction, the objection of multiple sciences sharing one subject will not arise, but another well-known objection will occur. The objection is that when you make ‘from the perspective of health and illness’ a restriction in medicine, or ‘from the perspective of motion and rest’ a restriction in natural science, then it necessarily implies that health and illness themselves, and motion and rest, are not discussed, but rather the attributes that attach from that aspect are discussed, because discussing health and illness, motion and rest would necessitate self-precedence, which is impossible. The reason is that then the attributes are also health and illness, and the medium of their application is also health and illness itself, so self-precedence would follow. Two answers have been given. The well-known answer is that the aspect and medium are possibility and readiness for health and illness, while the attributes are health and illness themselves. Apply analogy to motion and rest; thus self-precedence does not arise because possibility of health and health itself are different. The investigative answer is that what is meant when it is said, for example, that the subject of medicine is the human body with respect to health and illness, is that in all discussions the universal meaning of the aspect is preserved, not the particular health and illness; and the attributes that attach are particulars and individuals, so thus the mode of medium and the thing mediated are different. The commentator said: His statement: ‘And among them is the well-known [objection] etc. The third discussion on the permissibility of different sciences sharing one subject in essence and consideration, just as the opponents opposed the permissibility of multiple subjects for one science, similarly they opposed the impossibility of one subject for multiple sciences, and he claimed its permissibility, nay, its occurrence. As for the permissibility, it is because it is valid for one thing to have diverse essential attributes, i.e., different in kind; one science investigates some of its types, another science investigates some other types, so the two sciences or the sciences are distinguished by the attributes investigated even if the subject is the same. That is because the unity and difference of a science is only according to the informations, i.e., the issues, and just as the issues are united by the unity of their subjects by all returning to the subject of the science, and differ by their difference, so also they are united by the unity of their predicates by all returning to a type of the essential attributes of the subject and differ by their difference. Thus just as difference of sciences is considered by difference of subjects, it is permissible to consider difference by difference of predicates by taking one subject in essence and consideration and making the investigation of some of its essential attributes one science, and of others another science, so they become two sciences sharing in the subject.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage from Dars-e-Talweeh (a commentary on al-Tawdīḥ wa al-Talwīḥ) addresses two interrelated issues in defining the subject of a science. First, the problem of the 'aspect' (haythiyya) used to restrict the subject: if we say the subject of medicine is the human body 'from the perspective of health and illness', then health and illness themselves cannot be the accidental properties investigated, because that would cause circularity (tashrīf). Two solutions are offered: the popular one interprets the aspect as possibility/readiness (imkān/isti‘dād), while the investigative one treats the aspect as a universal concept (mafhūm kullī) that guides the discussions, not a specific attribute. The second issue, introduced as 'al-mabḥath al-thālith', argues that multiple sciences can indeed share the same subject (e.g., both medicine and psychology could study the human body) as long as they investigate different essential attributes (a‘rāḍ dhātiyya). The commentator (shāriḥ) supports this by logical reasoning: the identity of a science depends on its issues (masā’il) and their predicates (maḥmūlāt), not solely on the subject.

Key Terms
الحيثية (al-ḥaythiyya): The aspect or perspective that is used to restrict the subject of a science; it is a universal concept not a particular attribute
تشارك العلوم (tashāruk al-‘ulūm): Multiple sciences sharing the same subject in essence but distinguished by the attributes they investigate
الأعراض الذاتية (al-a‘rāḍ al-dhātiyya): Essential attributes that inherently belong to the subject and are investigated within a science

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'mashhūr' (well-known) answer to the objection that the haythiyya itself would be excluded from investigation?
A) The haythiyya is a universal concept observed in all discussions
B) The haythiyya refers to possibility and readiness, not actual health/illness
C) The subject is defined solely by its essence without any restriction
D) The haythiyya is itself one of the essential attributes and thus investigated

Key Takeaway
The haythiyya is a universal readiness concept that restricts the subject of a science without being an investigated attribute, and multiple sciences can share a subject as long as they investigate different essential attributes.

Method of Learning
intermediate

Can Multiple Sciences Share One Subject? The Hanafi View
Explaining the permissibility and actuality of multiple sciences sharing one subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on subject-sharing across sciences and apply the distinction between subject and predicate to classify sciences.

Core Idea
One subject can serve many sciences: diversity of essential attributes distinguishes them, not the subject alone.

This lesson examines a foundational usul debate: can multiple sciences share the same subject? The Hanafi school argues yes, both in possibility and reality, using the distinction between subject and predicate. Understanding this principle clarifies how Islamic sciences are classified and distinguished from one another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Issue
Recognize that the majority (al-qawm) held that a single subject cannot serve multiple sciences, while the author (Hanafi) asserts both possibility and occurrence.
The majority likely includes mutakallimun who define a science strictly by its subject.
2
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi position claims: (1) it is possible for multiple sciences to share one subject, and (2) it actually occurs in reality.
Possibility is argued by logical proof, occurrence by example.
3
Analyze the Proof for Possibility
A single thing can have diverse essential attributes (a'rad dhatiyya). If one science studies some attributes and another studies other attributes, they are distinguished by predicates (mahmulat) even if the subject is one.
Sciences can differ by predicates as well as by subjects.
4
Analyze the Proof for Occurrence
Example: astronomy ('ilm al-hay'a) and the science of heaven and the world ('ilm as-sama' wa al-'alam) both study the bodies of the world, but astronomy investigates shapes, while the other investigates natures.
Thus they are distinct sciences sharing a subject.
5
Recognize the Practical Implication
The classification of sciences can be based on predicates (attributes investigated) as well as subjects, allowing for multiple sciences to legitimately overlap in subject matter.
This affects how we organize knowledge in curricula and encyclopedias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : قوله : " ومنها أن المشهور ... إلخ المبحث الثالث في جواز تشارك العلوم المختلفة في موضوع واحد بالذات والاعتبار كما خالف القوم في جواز تعدد الموضوع لعلم واحد ، كذلك خالفهم في امتناع اتحاد الموضوع لعلوم متعدد وأدعى وازه بل وقوعه . أما الحواز فلأنه يصح أن يكون لشئ واحد أعراض ذاتية متنوعة أى : مختلفة بالنوع يبحث في علم عن بعض أنواعها ، وفى علم آخر عن بعض آخر ، فيتمايز العلمان أو العلوم بالأعراض المبحوث عنه ، وإن اتحد الموضوع وذلك ، لأن اتحاد العلم واختلافه إنما هو بحسب المعلومات ، أعنى المسائل وكما يتحد المسائل باتحاد موضـوعـاتـهـا بـأن يرجع الجميع إلى موضوع العلم ، ويختلف باختلافها كذلك بتحد باتحاد محمولاتها بأن يرجع الجميع إلى نوع من الأعراض الذاتية للموضوع ويختلف باختلافها ، فكما اعتبر اختلاف العلوم باختلاف الموضوعات ، يجوز أن يعتبر باختلاف المحمولات بأن يؤخذ موضوع واحد بالذات The commentator said: His words: 'And among them is that the well-known ... etc. The third research on the permissibility of different sciences sharing a single subject in essence and consideration. Just as he opposed the people (al-qawm) regarding the permissibility of multiple subjects for one science, similarly he opposed them regarding the impossibility of a single subject for multiple sciences, and he claimed its possibility, even its occurrence. As for the permissibility: because a single thing can have diverse essential attributes, i.e., different in type. One science investigates some of its types, and another science investigates others. Thus the two sciences or the sciences are distinguished by the attributes investigated, even if the subject is one. This is because the unity and difference of sciences depend on the information, i.e., the issues. Just as issues become one by the unity of their subjects, by returning all to the subject of the science, and differ by their difference, similarly they become one by the unity of their predicates, by returning all to a type of essential attribute of the subject, and differ by their difference. So just as the difference of sciences is considered by the difference of subjects, it is also permissible to consider it by the difference of predicates: a single subject is taken in essence and consideration, and investigation into its essential attributes is made one science, and into some others is made another science, so they become two sciences sharing in the subject and distinguished by the predicate. As for occurrence: they made the bodies of the world, which are the simple bodies, the subject of astronomy from the aspect of shape, and the subject of the science of heaven and the world from the aspect of nature. And the consideration therein is a statement of the essential attributes investigated for the parts of the subject; otherwise, they would not be investigated in the two sciences. So the subject of each of them is the bodies of the world absolutely, except that in astronomy the investigation is about their shapes, and in the science of heaven and the world about their natures. Thus they are two different sciences by the difference of the predicates of the issues while the subject is one. 'The science of heaven and the world' is a science in which are known the states of the bodies that are the pillars of the world: the heavens, the earth, the four elements, their natures, movements, positions, and the wisdom in their creation and execution. It is one of the divisions of natural science, which investigates the states of bodies from the aspect of change. Its subject is the sensible body as subject to change in states and stability therein, and it investigates what occurs to it from that aspect. Thus mentioned by Abu Ali. And it is not hidden that the consideration in natural science is investigated, and he has explicitly stated that it is a condition for attribution. Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage discusses a key usul principle about the subject of sciences. The author presents the third research (mabhas) concerning whether multiple sciences can share a single subject. He opposes the majority (al-qawm) who held that it is impossible for one subject to serve multiple sciences. The author argues for both the permissibility and actual occurrence. First, for permissibility: a single thing can have multiple 'essential attributes' (a'rad dhatiyya) that are different in kind. If one science studies some of these attributes and another science studies others, they are distinguished by what they investigate, even though the subject is the same. This is because the unity or difference of sciences depends not only on the subject but also on the predicates (mahmulat) of their issues. Just as sciences can differ by subject, they can also differ by predicate. Second, for actual occurrence: he gives the example of astronomy ('ilm al-hay'a') and the science of heaven and the world ('ilm as-sama' wa al-'alam'). Both have as their subject the bodies of the world (ajsam al-'alam), but astronomy studies them from the perspective of shape, while the other studies them from the perspective of nature. Thus they are two distinct sciences sharing a subject. The Urdu commentary clarifies that the author opposes the majority's view and asserts that one subject can serve multiple sciences, giving the same example. This illustrates the broader hanafi usul principle that the subject of a science is not the sole determinant; the predicates and attributes investigated also matter.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): In this passage, the subject (mawdu') of a science is the thing whose essential attributes are investigated in that science. It is the fundamental entity that the science studies.
عوارض ذاتية (a'rad dhatiyya): Essential attributes that are intrinsic to the subject and are the objects of investigation in a science.
محمول (mahmul): Predicate; in the context of sciences, it refers to the attribute that is attributed to the subject in the issues. Difference in predicates can distinguish sciences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what allows two sciences to be distinct despite sharing the same subject?
A) The different methods used in each science.
B) The different predicates (attributes studied) in each science.
C) The different geographical origins of the sciences.
D) The different time periods in which they were developed.

Key Takeaway
A single subject can serve multiple sciences; diversity of essential attributes investigated (predicates) allows distinction.

Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
One Subject, Multiple Sciences: The Hanafi Thesis
Establishes the possibility and occurrence of one subject for multiple sciences.

Learning Objective
State the Hanafi position that one subject can serve multiple sciences and explain the rational example of astronomy and physics.

Core Idea
One subject yields multiple sciences when different essential accidents are investigated.

In this passage, the commentator explains the author's position that a single subject can be the foundation for various sciences, contradicting the majority view. Using the example of astronomy and physics, he shows that while both study the bodies of the world, they differ in the aspects investigated—shape versus nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
What does the author assert about one subject for multiple sciences? The author claims it is both possible and actual.
The author opposes the majority (Jumhoor) who say one subject cannot serve multiple sciences.
2
Understand the rational example
How do astronomy and physics share the same subject but remain distinct sciences? Astronomy studies shapes, physics studies natures; both study the bodies of the world.
The example demonstrates that distinction lies in the accidents (predicates), not the subject.
3
Grasp the principle
Sciences are differentiated by their predicates (accidents), not necessarily by their subjects. A single subject can have multiple essential accidents, each giving rise to a distinct science.
This principle is key to understanding how usul al-fiqh relates to other sciences.

Distinguished by the predicate. As for occurrence, they made the bodies of the world, which are the simples, the subject of astronomy in terms of shape, and the subject of the science of the heavens and the world in terms of nature. And the 'aspect' [al-haythiyya] therein is an exposition of the essential accidents investigated for the parts of the subject, otherwise the investigation of them would not occur in the two sciences. So the subject of each of them is the bodies of the world absolutely, except that in astronomy the investigation is about their shapes, and in the science of the heavens and the world about their natures. Thus they are two different sciences by the difference of the predicates of the issues despite the unity of the subject. 'The science of the heavens and the world' is a science in which are known the states of the bodies that are the pillars of the world—the heavens, the earth, and the four elements—their natures, motions, positions, and the wisdom in their creation and execution. It is a branch of natural science, which investigates the states of bodies from the aspect of change. Its subject is the sensible body insofar as it is subject to change and stability, and it investigates what occurs to it from that aspect. Thus mentioned by Abu Ali. And it is not hidden that the aspect in natural science is investigated, and he stated that it is a restriction for the occurrence. Commentary: From here he is quoting the third investigation of the author regarding the subject. He says: Just as the author opposed the permissibility of multiple subjects for one science, likewise he opposes the majority regarding their statement that one subject cannot be for multiple sciences. According to the author, it is not only possible but also actually occurs that one subject serves multiple sciences. Possibility is in that one subject can have different accidents, so it can be that in one science some accidents are discussed and in another science other accidents, so the subject is one yet distinction is made by the difference of predicates and accidents. Occurrence is that the subject of astronomy is the bodies of the world and the subject of the science of the heavens and the world is also the bodies of the world, but in astronomy discussion is about the shapes of bodies, and in the science of the heavens and the world about their natures. So the subject is one, but distinction remains due to difference of accidents. What is the definition of the science of the heavens and the world? He says: The science of the heavens and the world is a science in which are known the states of the pillars of the world—the sky, earth, and the four elements—fire, air, water, and soil, and likewise their motions, composition, and the wisdoms in their arrangement. This science is in fact a branch of natural science. The subject of natural science is the sensible body from the aspect of accepting change and stability. So from the aspect of accepting change and stability, the accidents that occur to the body are investigated. Thus mentioned by Ibn Sina. It should be noted that the aspect mentioned in a science, according to the author, is for the exposition of accidents, and according to the commentator, it is a restriction for the occurrence. As the commentator said: There is a point of consideration here: First, this is based on what was mentioned about the aspect being sometimes a part of the subject and sometimes an exposition of the subject, and you know what is in that. Second, when they attempted to know the states of the entities of existing things, they assigned types and genera to realities and investigated what they encompassed of their essential accidents, so there resulted for them many issues unified in being an investigation of the states of that subject, even if their predicates differed. Thus they made it, by this consideration, one science singled out by codification and naming, and they allowed each one to add to it what he discovers of the states of that subject. For what is considered in a science is the investigation of all the essential accidents of the subject that human capability encompasses. So there is no meaning for a single science except that something or proportionate things be posited and all their essential accidents be investigated and sought. And there is no meaning for the differentiation of sciences except that one looks at the states of something and another at the states of something else different in essence or by consideration, meaning that in one science it is taken absolutely, and in the other restricted, or in each it is taken restricted by a different restriction. Those states are unknown and sought, while the subject is known among existing things, so it is fit as a cause for differentiation. Third, there is no science except that its subject includes diverse essential accidents, so each person can make it multiple sciences by this consideration. For example, one could make the investigation of the act of the obligated person from the aspect of obligation a science, and from the aspect of prohibition another science, and so on. Then fiqh would become multiple sciences with the subject being the act of the obligated person, and it would not be delimited.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a discussion in the commentary (sharh) of Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih on the compatibility of a single subject for multiple sciences. The author (Sadr al-Shariah) argues that it is possible and occurs, opposing the majority view that one subject cannot serve as the foundation for more than one science. The commentary elaborates with examples: astronomy ('ilm al-hay'a) studies the shapes of celestial bodies, while physics ('ilm al-sama' wa al-'alam) studies their natures. Both share the subject 'bodies of the world,' but differ in the accidents (predicates) they investigate. The commentator then notes three points of critique: first, the earlier claim about the 'aspect' (haythiyya) being part of the subject or its exposition is problematic; second, the single subject approach leads to a unified science gathering all issues about that subject; third, every subject has diverse accidents, so one could split a discipline like fiqh into multiple sciences (e.g., obligation, prohibition), making it unmanageable. The commentary supports the author's position that distinction of sciences lies in the difference of predicates, not necessarily in the subject. The Hanafi usul context here is foundational: the definition of 'ilm (science) and its subject matter, which is key to demarcating usul al-fiqh from other sciences. The example of fiqh's subject (act of the obligated person) shows how this principle applies to Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; the entity whose essential accidents are investigated in that science.
محمول (maḥmūl): The predicate or accident of the subject that is studied; it distinguishes one science from another when the subject is shared.
عرض (ج. أعراض) (ʿaraḍ (pl. aʿrāḍ)): Accident or attribute that occurs to the subject and is investigated in a science. Essential accidents (al-aʿrāḍ al-dhātīyya) are the proper objects of study.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for distinguishing astronomy and physics despite sharing the same subject?
A) They use different research methods
B) They investigate different predicates (shape vs. nature)
C) They were discovered by different scholars
D) They have different practical applications

Key Takeaway
One subject can yield multiple sciences when different essential accidents (predicates) are investigated.

Method of Learning
advanced

Critiques of the Single-Subject Thesis and Its Implications for Fiqh
Presents and critiques the single-subject thesis with three arguments.

Learning Objective
Explain the three critical points raised by the commentator and evaluate how they affect the unity or multiplicity of sciences like fiqh.

Core Idea
If fiqh were split by each accident, it would multiply indefinitely—an unsustainable fragmentation.

The commentator (Taftazani) raises three objections to the thesis that one subject can support multiple sciences. He argues that the 'aspect' earlier defined is problematic, that a science should cover all essential accidents of its subject, and that applying the logic to fiqh would lead to endless subdivision, rendering it unmanageable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first critique
The 'aspect' (haythiyya) is sometimes considered part of the subject, sometimes an exposition; this inconsistency is problematic.
This ambiguity affects how we define the scope of a science.
2
Understand the second critique
A science should investigate all essential accidents of its subject, not just a subset. The example of natural science shows that it studies the sensible body from all aspects of change and stability.
This contradicts the idea of splitting a subject's accidents into multiple sciences.
3
Grasp the third critique
If we allow each accident to form a separate science, then fiqh (subject: act of the obligated person) would split into countless sciences based on each hukm (obligation, prohibition, etc.), making it unmanageable.
This shows a practical limit to the principle.
4
Assess the implication
Does this critique refute the author's thesis? Not entirely. It suggests that while one subject can theoretically yield multiple sciences, practical and conceptual coherence often demand a unified science covering all essential accidents.
This is a refinement, not a rejection.
